When Controls Break Down

Guided Notes · Understanding Cancer at the Cellular Level

🔬 Section 1 — The Normal Cell Cycle
What You Will Learn in This Section
  • Name the four phases of the cell cycle (G1, S, G2, M) and describe what happens in each
  • Explain what a cell cycle checkpoint is and why the cell needs quality-control pause points
  • Identify the G1 checkpoint protein (p53) and describe its role as the cell's main damage detector

Label the four phases on the diagram, then complete the descriptions. Use the Word Bank.

✓ = checkpoint
PhaseWhat happens in this phase?
G1
S
G2
M
A is a quality-control pause point in the cell cycle. The protein guards the G1 checkpoint and is sometimes called the " of the genome."
Why This Matters

Understanding which phase DNA replication happens in is the foundation for everything that follows. When replication goes wrong and errors slip through, the cell's genetic instructions are corrupted. If those errors are not caught at a checkpoint, they get copied into every daughter cell. That accumulation across generations of cells is how a single mutation becomes thousands — and how cancer begins.

📖 Section 2 — Key Definitions
What You Will Learn in This Section
  • Define tumor suppressor gene and oncogene in your own words, with examples from this lesson
  • Explain apoptosis and whether it is beneficial or harmful to the body
  • Define metastasis and explain why it makes cancer harder to treat

Why This Matters

These four terms are the vocabulary of cancer research. When you read a news headline about a new treatment or hear a doctor describe a diagnosis, you will hear these words. Understanding them now means you can follow the science — and the stories of real patients — for the rest of your life.

🚗 Section 3 — The Car Analogy
What You Will Learn in This Section
  • Apply the car analogy to explain the difference between gain-of-function and loss-of-function mutations
  • Identify which type of mutation produces an oncogene and which produces an inactivated tumor suppressor
  • Explain why having both a stuck accelerator and no brakes is more dangerous than either alone
Complete the analogy using what you know about the cell cycle
Think of a cancer cell like a car with a (always sending "divide" signals, can't be turned off) and (checkpoint protein is inactivated, can't slow cell division down).
The "Stuck Accelerator" in biology is called a(n):
It forms when a gets a gain-of-function mutation.
The "No Brakes" in biology is a mutated:
A key example in this lesson: (TP53)
Why This Matters

The distinction between gain-of-function and loss-of-function mutations is not just conceptual — it determines the treatment approach. A drug that blocks an overactive RAS protein (stuck accelerator) is mechanistically different from a therapy that compensates for lost p53 function (no brakes). These are different problems that require different solutions. This is exactly why identifying the type of mutation in a patient's cancer matters clinically.

🎯 Section 4 — The Two-Hit Hypothesis
What You Will Learn in This Section
  • Explain why both copies of a tumor suppressor gene must be mutated for the checkpoint to fail
  • Apply the two-hit hypothesis to predict why cancer usually requires more than one mutation
  • Connect the two-hit hypothesis to inherited cancer risk and why it matters for screening

Complete the diagram showing how a tumor suppressor gene is inactivated, then answer the question below.

Starting State
Copy 1: Normal ✓
Copy 2: Normal ✓

→

Copy 1: Mutated ✗
Copy 2: Normal ✓
Result:
→
After 2nd Hit
Copy 1: Mutated ✗
Copy 2: Mutated ✗
Result:
Why does cancer usually require MORE than one mutation? Use this diagram as evidence.
🌐 Language Support — Sentence Starter
When a cell has one mutated copy of a tumor suppressor gene, it ________________ because the other copy ________________. Cancer occurs when both copies are mutated because then the cell ________________.
Why This Matters

The two-hit hypothesis explains why inherited cancer predisposition is clinically significant. A person born with one already-mutated copy of a tumor suppressor gene needs only one more mutation to completely lose that checkpoint — a much shorter path to cancer than someone who starts with two functional copies. This is why genetic screening programs at children's cancer hospitals focus on identifying families who carry inherited mutations before cancer develops.

  • Compare outcomes across scenarios to identify which combination is most dangerous and why
  • Apply your observations directly to the Maya case study in the next section

Why This Matters

Simulators let you test what scientists cannot always test ethically in a living person — you can run the same cell under four different genetic conditions and compare outcomes. This is how real computational biologists at research hospitals model cancer: they change one variable, observe the effect, and use those observations to predict how a patient's cells will behave. What you just did is, at its core, how modern precision medicine begins.

👤 Section 6 — Case Study: Meet Maya
What You Will Learn in This Section
  • Apply everything from Sections 1–5 to analyze a real pediatric leukemia case
  • Identify which checkpoint failed in Maya's cells and support your reasoning with evidence from the case clues
  • Connect the cellular biology you learned to why understanding specific mutations matters for treatment
Patient Case File

"Maya is 12 years old. She was recently diagnosed with Acute Lymphoblastic Leukemia (ALL)."

📋Lab report shows an abnormally high white blood cell count — lymphoblasts (immature white blood cells) are present in large numbers in the blood
🔬Bone marrow biopsy confirms lymphoblasts in uncontrolled division — normal red blood cell production is being crowded out
🧬Genetic panel reveals a mutation in a tumor suppressor gene that normally regulates lymphoblast development
❓Your team must answer: Which checkpoint failed — and what does the evidence tell you?
Case Analysis QuestionYour Response
Based on the clues, which cell cycle checkpoint most likely failed in Maya's cells?
🌐 Language Support — Sentence Starter
The checkpoint that most likely failed in Maya's cells is the ________________ checkpoint. I know this because Clue ____ shows that ________________. The protein that should have stopped this is called ________________.
What type of mutation caused this — gain-of-function or loss-of-function? How do you know?
🌐 Language Support — Sentence Starter
This is a ________________-of-function mutation because the gene ________________ instead of ________________. The evidence from the genetic panel shows ________________, which supports the two-hit hypothesis because ________________.
Why are Maya's white blood cells specifically affected, not her skin or muscle cells?
If you were a pediatric cancer researcher, what would you want to know first about Maya's specific mutation?
Why This Matters

Maya's case represents the biology of the most common childhood cancer. Advances in pediatric cancer research have helped bring the survival rate for ALL from under 10% in the 1960s to over 90% today — and that progress came from understanding the specific cellular and genetic mechanisms driving the disease. Knowing which checkpoint failed and which gene is mutated determines which treatment approach can target it most precisely.

🔗 Section 7 — Connecting It All
What You Will Learn in This Section
  • Synthesize all six sections into a coherent explanation of cancer at the cellular level
  • Use evidence to evaluate a common misconception about inherited cancer
  • Connect the biology of checkpoint mutations to the broader goal of targeted cancer treatment

Answer in complete sentences. Use at least three vocabulary words from the Word Bank in each response.

1. In your own words, explain what cancer is at the cellular level. Use at least three vocabulary words.
🌐 Language Support — Sentence Starter
When a cell's checkpoint fails, the cell ________________ instead of ________________. This leads to ________________ growth because ________________. Over time, this can cause cancer because ________________.
2. A student says: "All cancer is inherited — you get it from your parents." Do you agree or disagree? Use evidence from this lesson.
3. Advances in pediatric cancer research have brought ALL survival from under 10% (1960s) to over 90% today. Why would understanding the specific mutations in a child's cancer help researchers design better treatments?
Research Connection — After the Video Clip
How does the researcher's work connect to what you learned about cell cycle checkpoints and cancer biology?
One thing that surprised you or that you want to learn more about:
Why This Matters

Scientists are not just people who memorize vocabulary — they are people who can connect ideas across different experiments, patients, and scales. Synthesizing what you learned about checkpoints, mutations, the two-hit hypothesis, and Maya's story into one coherent explanation is exactly the thinking skill a cancer researcher uses every day. This is why the final question is not "what is cancer?" but "how would you explain it?"
